security || This patch is a security patch (SRP). 

The patch addresses a potential NULL pointer dereference issue. A NULL pointer dereference happens when a program attempts to use a pointer that is not pointing to a valid object, which can lead to program crashes or unexpected behavior. In some cases, it can be exploited to execute arbitrary code, which is a serious security concern. 

In this patch, the author adds a check to ensure that the pointer 'data->socdata' is not NULL before it is used. If it is NULL, an error message is printed and the function returns, preventing the NULL pointer dereference. This is a typical example of a security patch as it fixes a potential vulnerability in the software.

security || This patch is (A) a security patch (SRP).

The patch changes the use of strncpy() to strlcpy(). Both are string copying functions, but strncpy() can lead to buffer overflow vulnerabilities if not used correctly, as it does not null-terminate the destination string if the source string is longer than the specified length. On the other hand, strlcpy() always null-terminates the destination string, which can prevent such vulnerabilities. Therefore, this patch is addressing a potential security issue, making it a security patch.

In the patched code, a check is added to see if `c->dv_fctx` is NULL, which would indicate that the memory allocation failed. If it is NULL, an error message is logged and an error code is returned. This prevents further use of the `c->dv_fctx` pointer, which could lead to undefined behavior or a crash if the allocation failed.

This kind of patch is considered a security patch because it addresses a potential vulnerability where a failure in memory allocation could lead to unexpected behavior or system instability, which could be exploited maliciously.

security || This patch is a security patch (SRP). 

The patch addresses a segmentation fault (segfault) issue that occurs when the program tries to copy a NULL 'ce_tag'. A segmentation fault is a specific kind of error caused by accessing memory that "does not belong to you." It's a mechanism that prevents you from corrupting the memory and introducing hard-to-debug memory bugs. Whenever you get a segmentation fault, you know you are doing something wrong with memory  such as accessing a variable that has already been freed or writing to a read-only portion of memory. The consequences of such actions can potentially be dangerous and can lead to unexpected behavior, crashes, or even allow for the execution of malicious code. 

The patch adds a check to ensure 'ce_tag' is not NULL before attempting to copy it, thus preventing the segfault. This is a security-related fix as it addresses a potential vulnerability in the software.

The difference between them is that 'xmalloc' is a version of 'malloc' that exits if the allocation fails, while 'malloc' returns a NULL pointer if it fails. 

This change does not directly address any security vulnerabilities, weaknesses, or potential exploits within the software system. It seems to be more about improving the robustness of the code and handling potential memory allocation failures more gracefully. Therefore, it is not a security patch.

Use-after-free vulnerabilities are a type of security issue where the program continues to use a pointer after it has been freed. This can lead to a variety of security problems, including execution of arbitrary code, which can be exploited by an attacker.

security || This patch is a Security Patch (SRP). 

The patch addresses a potential memory corruption issue in the 'ath10k' wireless driver. The problem arises when the tx ring index is immediately trimmed with a bitmask, which discards the 0xFFFFFFFF error case. This error case can theoretically occur when a device is abruptly disconnected, leading to the use of an invalid tx ring index and potentially causing memory corruption. The patch fixes this by adding a check for the 0xFFFFFFFF error case and returning an error if it occurs, thus preventing the use of an invalid tx ring index and the potential memory corruption.

The commit message "PNIO: Fix another potential OOB write" indicates that the patch is intended to fix a potential Out-Of-Bounds (OOB) write issue. An OOB write is a security vulnerability that could allow an attacker to write data outside of the intended boundaries of an array or similar data structures in memory. This could lead to a variety of security issues, including the execution of arbitrary code, which could compromise the system. 

In the code changes, we can see that the patch modifies how memory is allocated and how data is copied into 'moduleNameStr'. The use of 'g_strlcpy' and the change in 'sscanf' function indicate that the patch is designed to prevent more characters than the buffer can hold from being copied, which is a common cause of OOB issues. 

Therefore, based on the purpose of the patch and the nature of the code changes, this patch can be classified as a security patch.
security || This patch is (B) a non-security patch (NSP).

The patch changes the memory allocation function from kzalloc to kcalloc in the rtl8188eu driver. Both kzalloc and kcalloc are used for memory allocation in the kernel, but they are used in different scenarios. kzalloc is used when you know the total size of memory you want to allocate, while kcalloc is used when you want to allocate memory for an array of objects, and you know the number of elements and the size of each element.

The patch is modifying the m_ssl_gnutls.cpp file, which is related to the SSL (Secure Sockets Layer) protocol. SSL is a security protocol used to establish encrypted links between a web server and a browser, ensuring that all data passed between them remains private and integral.

The specific change in this patch is adding a condition to check if 'dh_alloc' is not initialized before generating Diffie-Hellman parameters with 'gnutls_dh_params_generate2'. Diffie-Hellman is a method of securely exchanging cryptographic keys over a public channel, and it's crucial to the SSL protocol.

If 'dh_alloc' is not initialized, the function returns without generating the parameters. This could prevent potential issues such as memory leaks or crashes that might occur if the function tried to generate parameters with an uninitialized 'dh_alloc'. 

Therefore, this patch is addressing a potential vulnerability in the SSL implementation, making it a security patch.

This is a common way to handle memory allocation errors and can help prevent potential security vulnerabilities.

It's a mechanism that prevents you from corrupting the memory and introducing hard-to-debug memory bugs. security || This patch is a security patch (SRP). 

The patch is checking the return value of the calloc function, which is a memory allocation function. If calloc fails to allocate the requested memory, it returns a NULL pointer. The patch adds a check for this condition and if it occurs, an error message "malloc: out of memory" is set and the function exits. 

This is a security-related change because failing to check the result of a memory allocation can lead to a null pointer dereference, which is a common security vulnerability. If an attacker can trigger a condition where calloc fails, they might be able to exploit the null pointer dereference to execute arbitrary code or cause a denial of service.

security || This patch is a security patch (SRP). The patch is addressing a potential use-after-free error, which is a type of security vulnerability. security || This patch is a security patch (SRP). 

The patch is applied to the 'evm_crypto.c' file, which is part of the security/integrity/evm module. EVM stands for Extended Verification Module, a Linux security module that detects tampering of security-sensitive files. 

The patch addresses a race condition during the allocation of a cryptographic hash function (tfm). Race conditions are a type of vulnerability that can lead to unpredictable behavior, including potential security issues. 

In this case, the patch introduces a mutex lock to prevent simultaneous access to the 'hmac_tfm' variable, which could lead to a race condition. This is a common technique for preventing race conditions in multithreaded programming. 

Therefore, given that it addresses a potential vulnerability in a security module, this patch can be classified as a security patch (SRP).

security || This patch is a non-security patch (NSP). 

The patch addresses a segmentation fault (segfault) bug in the representation of extension class methods. A segfault is a specific kind of error caused by accessing memory that "does not belong to you." It's a mechanism that prevents you from corrupting the memory and introducing hard-to-debug memory bugs. Whenever you get a segfault, you know you are doing something wrong with memory  such as accessing a variable that has already been freed or writing to a read-only portion of memory. The fix provided in this patch ensures that the system doesn't crash when it encounters this issue.

While this does improve the stability of the system, it doesn't directly address any security vulnerabilities, weaknesses, or potential exploits. Therefore, it's not classified as a security patch.

non-security || This patch appears to be a Security Patch (SRP). 

The commit message indicates that the patch is intended to fix a segmentation fault (segv) that occurs when the 'snoop' tool is decoding LDAP traffic. A segmentation fault is a specific kind of error caused by accessing memory that "does not belong to you." It's a mechanism that prevents you from corrupting the memory and introducing hard-to-debug memory bugs. Whenever you get a segmentation fault, you know you are doing something wrong with memory  such as accessing a variable that has already been freed or writing to a read-only portion of memory. The fact that this patch fixes a segv implies that it is addressing a potential vulnerability, hence it can be classified as a security patch.

non-security || This patch is (A) a security patch (SRP).

The patch replaces the use of the mktemp function with mkstemp. The mktemp function generates a unique temporary filename, but it has a race condition that can be exploited: an attacker could potentially create a file with the same name between the time mktemp checks for the existence of the same file and the time the program opens the file. This could lead to a variety of security vulnerabilities, such as unauthorized file access or denial of service.

On the other hand, mkstemp creates a unique temporary file and opens it atomically, which eliminates the race condition, making it a safer choice. The patch also includes a call to fchmod to set the file permissions, which is another security-related change. Therefore, this patch is addressing a security concern and can be classified as a security patch.

The switch from `copy_from_user()` to `__copy_from_user()` is also a security-related change. The patch replaces the strcpy function with strlcpy. The strlcpy function, on the other hand, takes the size of the destination buffer as an argument and ensures that it does not write past the end of the buffer, thus preventing potential buffer overflow vulnerabilities. Therefore, this patch is addressing a potential security vulnerability and can be classified as a security patch.

The fact that it was previously allowed on regular files could potentially lead to unauthorized data modification or other security issues. 

The patch fixes this by adding a condition to check if the method used is POST, and if so, it logs an error and returns an HTTP_METHOD_NOT_ALLOWED status. This prevents the misuse of the POST method on regular files, thereby enhancing the security of the system.

non-security || This patch is a security patch (SRP). 

The patch addresses a potential vulnerability in the memory management subsystem of the Linux kernel. The function `fixup_user_fault()` is modified to check the virtual memory area (vma) access rights. This is a security-related change because it prevents unauthorized access to memory areas. 

Before the patch, the function relied on `handle_mm_fault()` to map the page, but it didn't guarantee that the page would be mapped with sufficient permissions. This could potentially lead to unauthorized memory access, a common security issue. 

The patch adds a check to ensure that the memory page has the correct access rights, thus mitigating the potential security risk.

non-security || This patch appears to be a security patch (SRP). 

The changes made in this patch are in the file "phishcheck.c", which is likely related to phishing checks. The modifications are specifically in the function "cli_url_canon", which seems to be responsible for canonicalizing or standardizing URLs. 

The patch changes how the function handles URLs that contain a username (preceded by an '@' symbol). Before the patch, the function simply found the first '@' symbol and ignored everything before it. After the patch, the function first skips any leading slashes, then it determines the length of the host name (up to a colon, slash, or question mark), and only then does it look for the '@' symbol within that length. 

This change could prevent potential security issues where a URL is crafted in a way that tricks the original function into ignoring parts of the URL that it shouldn't. This could be used in phishing attacks, where an attacker tries to trick a user into thinking a URL points to a trusted site when it actually points to a malicious site. Therefore, this patch is likely a security patch.

non-security || This patch is (B) a non-security patch (NSP). 

The patch is a revert of a previous commit that was causing issues with the kexec with certain drivers. The problem was that the shutdown sequence was putting the hardware into D3 sleep, and the commit was causing the system to claim it was in D0 (running) state. This was causing the device to not wake up properly. 

This patch does not address any security vulnerabilities, weaknesses, or potential exploits. It is more related to the functionality and performance of the system, rather than its security. Therefore, it is a non-security patch.
